Can the Council confirm that it received an opinion from its own Legal Service in December 1993 advising that a proposal for a Council Directive on the approximation of the laws, regulations and administrative provisions of the Member States on advertising for tobacco products could not be based on Article 100A or on any other Treaty Article and, further, can the Council confirm that the opinion was neither revised nor withdrawn before the meeting of the Health Council on 4 December 1997, at which agreement on a common position was reached?

Answer

1.The Council is assisted in its deliberations by a Legal Service, the role of which is to provide independent legal advice to the Council. These opinions are given in written or oral form either to the Council itself or to its preparatory organs. They are internal to the Council.

Question No 34 by Hans Lindqvist (H−0357/98)

Subject: EU stipulation of details ruining long‐distance public transport services

The Court of Justice has rejected an application from Storstockholms Lokaltrafik (SL) – Greater Stockholm Public Transport – for a derogation from EU transport rules on the grounds that SL Buss is not a public authority but a company. As SL Buss is the only organization running services on its routes and those services are supervised by a public authority, it should be possible for the EU derogation to apply. The derogation would mean that drivers would only have to produce duty sheets for one day's driving instead of for a week. If the EU is to interfere in the details of all kinds of rules and regulations in almost every conceivable area of society, it is hardly likely that circumstances will improve for authorities, companies or individuals. Is it not time to cut through the tangled web of directives and look beyond the boundaries of Strasbourg and Brussels and realize that completely different conditions apply to public transport services in the more sparsely populated metropolitan areas, such as in Sweden and Finland, from those applying in the central parts of the EU and that Community rules in these areas are inappropriate?

Answer

Whilst recalling that it is for the Commission to ensure that Community law is properly applied and for the Court of Justice to rule on its interpretation and application, the Council points out the following:

‐ Council Regulation (EEC) No 3820/85 aims at harmonizing social protection and road safety rules as well as conditions of competence within the Community;

‐ Article 13 of the said Regulation provides for exceptions so that different conditions prevailing in Member States can be taken into account;

‐ it is up to Member States to grant such exceptions;

‐ in the case referred to by the Honourable Member, the judgement of the Court of Justice is in line with the Swedish Government's decision not to grant such an exception.

Question No 35 by Olivier Dupuis (H−0359/98)

Subject: Terrorist networks in Europe

Representatives of the Algerian Government have condemned at a number of official meetings the freedom of action enjoyed in Europe by terrorist networks linked to the AIG, the AIS and other armed groups. During his visit to the European Parliament the Algerian Foreign Affairs Minister, Mr Attaf, condemned the fact that terrorist support groups, taking advantage of the climate of 'tolerance' in various countries of the Union, and particularly in Sweden and the United Kingdom, were free to carry on their activities in a number of European capitals and had set up what could only be described as logistical support, propaganda and recruitment bases. What steps has the Council taken, or does it intend to take, in order to combat effectively the Algerian terrorist networks active in Europe? And, in more general terms, what decisions has the Council taken, or will it take, to cooperate fully with the Algerian authorities in their fight against terrorism and the ongoing process of democratization in Algeria?

Answer

As the Honourable Member knows, the EU and Algeria agreed on 26 November 1997 in Luxembourg to maintain a continuing dialogue. A Ministerial Troika visited Algiers from 19 to 20 January 1998. The dialogue has also been pursued at parliamentary level by national Parliaments and the European Parliament itself which undertook a very successful visit to Algiers from 8 to 12 February 1998. We all agree that the on‐going dialogue must continue.

Let me make it clear that the European Union Member States have not been and are not havens for Algerian terrorist groups. Action against terrorism is a national responsibility, not that of the Council of Ministers – but let me reaffirm that in all EU Member States, action is taken whenever there is sufficient proof of crime to meet the requirements of national law. In this context, I salute the courage and efficiency of the Belgian police, who recently arrested eight suspected GIA members in Brussels after an exchange of fire. This operation, which is far from unique, confirms that accusations that EU capitals are havens for terrorists are unfounded.

I am glad to report that Interpol recently held a conference in Algiers to discuss the Algerian terrorist threat and how to counter it. EU Member States were strongly represented at this meeting, and made a decisive contribution to the debate. The European Union has repeatedly affirmed its interest in an inclusive, broad‐based dialogue with Algeria dealing with any concerns and proposals that the Algerian authorities might seek to bring to its attention, not least concerning the fight against terrorism and the ‚ongoing process of democratization’, to quote the Honourable Member. The Presidency is also looking at the best was to pursue a dialogue on such issues in the Euro‐Med context.

Question No 36 by Mihail Papayannakis (H−0363/98)

Subject: Greece's progress towards EMU

The Greek Government has announced the entry of the drachma into the Exchange Rate Mechanism accompanied by a 13.8 % devaluation. At the same time it has unveiled a package of measures and structural changes in the Greek economy. It has stated that all these initiatives form part of a comprehensive programme leading up to Greek membership of EMU in 2001.

Will the Council provide its own view of the negotiations which led to the adoption of this programme and say how it will lead Greece to membership of EMU by 2001?

Answer

The Council welcomes the successful conclusion of negotiations leading to the drachma"s entry into the exchange rate mechanism of the EMS.

The Council also welcomes the structural reforms announced by the Greek government.

The package should facilitate further convergence of the Greek economy and fulfilment of the necessary conditions for the adoption of the single currency.

Question No 37 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−0364/98)

Subject: US statements on the possibility of unilateral intervention in Iraq following the UN resolution

The latest UN Security Council resolution on Iraq provides for the harshest consequences for Iraq should Baghdad fail to respect the letter of the agreement it has signed with the UN Secretary‐General, Kofi Annan. Ambassador Bill Richardson, the US administration's representative at the UN, has stated that this resolution gives each member of the Security Council the right to take unilateral action, and a White House spokesman has added that the harshest consequences can mean only one thing: military action.

Does the Council share this interpretation by the US administration which allows the USA basically to replace international organizations and to assume the role of international policeman? If not, what political initiatives does it intend to take to defuse the crisis in Iraq, to strengthen the role of the UN and to promote a peaceful solution to international conflicts, so that certain powers cease to use the UN as a means for pursuing their own selfish interests?

Answer

It would not be appropriate for me, in my capacity of President‐in‐office of the Council, to comment on the interpretations given to Security Council Resolution 1154 by other Permanent Members of the Security Council.

I shall refer the Honourable Member to the Council's conclusions of 23 February in which the Council set out its views on the latest crisis with Iraq.

Question No 38 by Christine Oddy (H−0366/98)

Subject: Debt reduction and the Jubilee Campaign

What steps is the UK Presidency taking to reduce debt and to support the Jubilee campaign?

Answer

The UK, as presidency, is fully committed to the Heavily Indebted Poor Countries (HIPC) initiative that was launched by the IMF and World Bank in 1996. The UK Presidency supported an EU contribution to the HIPC initiative, which has now been fully agreed.

While the EU remains concerned about, and supports measures contributing to, debt relief, this is primarily an issue requiring multilateral action within the framework of the International Financial Institutions. The UK is actively seeking the full and speedy extension of relief to more countries in line with the HIPC initiative.

Question No 39 by Ludivina García Arias (H−0377/98)

Subject: Arrest of a group of Spanish extreme right‐wingers in Cologne (Germany)

Is the Council aware that 150 extreme right‐wing Real Madrid fans have been arrested in Cologne for shouting slogans, displaying swastikas and making arm salutes?

Does the Council intend to ask the Spanish government who funded these people's travel expenses, and what frontier controls were exercised to prevent them traveling to another Member State?

Does the Council not consider that in cases such as this it is best to recommend to the embassies that they should not take any measures to avoid legal proceedings, to make the extreme right‐wing groups realize that tough and coordinated action will be taken against anybody promoting the extreme right?

Answer

The Council is not aware of the facts referred to in this question and would like to draw the attention of the honourable Member to the fact that enforcement of law and order is of the exclusive competence of the authorities of the Member State concerned, in this instance police forces, and – should it be the case – of Germany's judicial system.

Question No 40 by Anne McIntosh (H−0149/98)

Subject: Kick‐back incentives to travel agents

What evidence does the Council have of 'kick‐back' commissions and other incentives being offered by European airlines to travel agents; what action does it intend to take against airlines pursuing such anti‐competitive practices?

Answer

In response to the oral question put by Ms Anne McIntosh, no specific case has been reported to the Council. The honourable parliamentarian is well aware that any specific anti‐competitive practice or behaviour by airlines or travel agents would be a matter falling within the Commission's competence and for appropriate examination and action by that Institution.

2.Questions to the Commission

 

Question No 44 by Lis Jensen (H−0354/98)

Subject: Right to strike and set up blockades

What conclusions does the Commission draw from the European Court of Justice's ruling on the French government's failure to take adequate action with regard to blockades that could prevent the free movement of goods. Does it share the view of the Council's Legal Service that the right to strike, the setting‐up of blockades and similar matters fall outside the scope of both the Maastricht Treaty and the Amsterdam Treaty?

Answer

The Court of Justice"s ruling of 9.12.1997(2) stated that failure by a Member State to take action against individuals who create obstacles to the free movement of goods is incompatible with Article 30 of the EC Treaty, in accordance with Article 5 of the Treaty. The Member State in question is therefore required to adopt the measures it considers necessary and appropriate, and in a given situation, most capable of removing the barriers to the import of goods.

The Commission would like to emphasize that economic operators affected by barriers to trade similar to those mentioned in the above ruling must bring an action before a national court, which must draw conclusions from the above ruling. If appropriate, the national judge must pronounce the Member State responsible for the losses sustained by the economic operator.

The Commission wishes to remind the Member that by adopting a proposal for a regulation introducing an intervention mechanism for the Commission to remove certain obstacles to trade(3) , it fulfilled a specific mandate from the Amsterdam European Council. The Commission believes that the proposed mechanism is potentially the most effective way of improving the present procedure, based on Article 169 of the EC Treaty, and therefore of reaching an effective solution within the scope of its mandate.

As the Commission stressed in its reply to Mr Schörling"s question(4) , there is no conflict between the intervention mechanism and the right to strike. The right to strike is protected under national laws and is not affected by the Commission"s proposal.

Question No 48 by John McCartin (H−0287/98)

Subject: Fishing by Dutch vessels off the West Coast of Ireland

Is the Commission aware of the allegations that Dutch vessels fishing off the west coast of Ireland are landing huge catches unmonitored in Las Palmas in the Canaries? What action will the Commission take to ensure that this illegal fishing activity is halted?

Answer

According to EC regulations, Member States are responsible for adopting appropriate measures for the inspection and monitoring of fishing activities.

Therefore, in the case referred to by the Member, the Member State, that is, the Netherlands, is responsible for the management of its fishing fleet in compliance with EC requirements. Ireland is responsible for the inspection and monitoring of fishing activities within its waters, including the activities of vessels flying the Dutch flag. The Member State in which the port is located, in this case Spain, is responsible for the inspection of fishery products landed in its ports, and for informing the Member State whose vessels are involved.

The Commission has only about twenty fishery inspectors and cannot by itself ensure compliance of every EC fishing fleet with EC requirements. In fact, the Commission"s inspectors carry out spot checks, in order to ensure that effective inspections are carried out by the competent authorities designated by the Member States.

In this respect, the Commission has monitored the inspections carried out in Las Palmas and has found no evidence to confirm the allegations to which the Member refers. Indeed, in this particular case, landings of fishery products in the port of Las Palmas are monitored by Spanish national fishery inspectors which are permanently based in Las Palmas.

According to informations available to the Commission, in 1997 two vessels flying the flag of the Netherlands operated in Western waters (ICES area VII) on their way to fishing ground off the coast of Northwest Africa. These vessels landed about 200 tonnes of horse mackerel and a small quantity of mackerel in Las Palmas. These vessels did not return to Western waters.

Question No 49 by James Provan (H−0300/98)

Subject: BSE

What data does the Commission have on pathological screening for BSE across the EU – recognizing that it is being done in some countries as well as the UK?

Answer

1. In all Member States BSE is a notifiable disease. Member States have to report observed BSE cases within two weeks to the Commission. In addition, in situations where it concerns outbreaks in previously unaffected countries the Member States concerned report within the Standing Veterinary Committee to the Commission and Member States on all findings related to the epidemiological investigations.

2. All Member States have surveillance systems for BSE which, however, are different from country to country. Screening may be applied, in the framework of the BSE surveillance programme, to an epidemiologically representative sample of bovine brains collected after slaughter and/or on the animals that show symptoms of a neurological disease and where the suspicion of BSE exists. Sampling plans have been established by the Office International des Epizooties (OIE).

The presently available tests themselves are time and labour intensive; they need intensive laboratory work, including microscope examination. Therefore, these tests are not really adapted for application to large samples.

The Commission does not systematically receive the results of such screenings. However, Commission inspectors visited all Member States in 1996 to assess their BSE surveillance. The number of suspected animals which had undergone laboratory examination to exclude BSE was asked for by the inspectors. The reports of these missions were submitted to the European Parliament. Follow‐up missions were made in 1997 to Member States where major deficiencies of BSE surveillance were observed.

Furthermore, the Member States that have already submitted to the Commission an application to be considered free of BSE, report on their animal BSE surveillance systems and include in their application the results of the tests carried out so far. (These Member States are Denmark, Finland, Sweden, Germany and Austria). A number of other Member States also make their results available (for example Belgium).

The requirement for the Member States to present data on the results of BSE surveillance to the European Commission has been introduced by the Commission Decision on Epidemio‐surveillance for animal transmissible spongiform encephalopathies adopted in March 1998.

3. The Commission is aware of companies that are developing tests that go beyond pure research and which might have a scientific basis for tests BSE infectivity. The Commission is considering rapidly launching a programme for the validation of operational post‐mortem tests for the diagnosis of animal transmissible spongiform encephalopathies (especially BSE), with a view to their community approval as soon as possible.

Question No 50 by Josu Imaz San Miguel (H−0303/98)

Subject: Freedom of fisheries investment in the European Union

The UK Government is trying to adopt restrictive measures attaching conditions to investment in the UK by fishing companies from other EU countries. Over one hundred vessels belonging to shipowners from Galicia, the Basque Country, the Netherlands and other Community countries are faced with a threat from the UK Government requiring them to recruit half of their crew members from amongst UK nationals and to land a significant proportion of their catch in UK ports. These requirements run counter to the basic freedoms of business establishment and movement of goods within the European Union.

As guardian of the Treaties, what view does the Commission take of the options contained in the plans which the UK Government has already shown to the Commission? Is the Commission considering an appeal to the Court of Justice in Luxembourg if those plans are adopted in their existing form?

Answer

The Court of Justice has stated that EC law as it now stands, does not preclude a Member State, in authorizing one if its vessels to fish against national quotas, from laying down conditions to ensure that the vessel has a real economic link with that Member State if that link concerns only the relations between the vessel's fishing operations and the populations dependent on fisheries and related industries. Moreover such conditions must be alternative, nondiscriminatory and proportional to their aim.

It is against this background that the Commission will evaluate the compatibility with EC law of the UK draft or any other national proposal on economic link conditions.

The Commission in its role as guardian of the Treaties will ensure that both the enactment and the practical application of national economic link conditions fully respect Community law as interpreted by the Court of Justice.

The UK draft, to which the honourable MEP is referring in his question, has been the subject matter of extensive technical discussions between the Commission and the UK authorities, the Spanish authorities and representatives of ‚anglo‐spanish’ vessels.

The Commission will take a final position only on the basis of a final draft to be officially submitted by the UK authorities for consultation. This has not happened so far.

Question No 51 by Daniel Varela Suanzes‐Carpegna (H−0325/98)

Subject: Commission's position on the extraterritorial nature of Canada's fisheries bill C−27

With its fisheries bill C−27, Canada is continuing to operate extraterritorial provisions which are contrary to international law and, in particular, the international law of the sea, incorrectly interpreting the New York agreement on transzonal species, impinging on the Community's fishing interests in NAFO and undermining the bilateral EUCanada cooperation relations institutionalized in the 1996 agreement.

Can the Commission state its position on Canada's bill C−27 and on the effects of Canada's interpretation therein of the New York agreement on transzonal species and its repercussions for the existing NAFO rules and bilateral relations in general?

Answer

The Commission feels that a number of provisions contained in the proposed Canadian legislation to implement the UN Agreement on Straddling Stocks may be inconsistent with the Law of the Sea as it stands and may go beyond what was agreed in the UN framework. Consequently, this is a matter of serious concern for the Community. The Commission has consistently expressed such concern to the Canadian authorities whenever the opportunity occurred in the past. It will continue to follow closely the legislative process in Canada and, in further contacts and discussions with the Canadian side, it will emphasize the salient points at issue.

The Commission sees no direct implications for the process of ratifying the UN Agreement. On the contrary, events like the Canadian bill seem to plead for an accelerated ratification process so that dispute settlement can take place as quickly as possible in order to iron out any dissenting views concerning the interpretation and application of the UN Agreement.

It is clear that it would facilitate bilateral fisheries relations if Canada were to take the Community's comments on board and to come forth with an improved piece of legislation.

Question No 52 by Peter Crampton (H−0373/98)

Subject: Fisheries agreement with South Africa

Negotiations have begun between the European Union and South Africa for the conclusion of a fisheries agreement.

During these negotiations, is the Commission taking into account the need to move away from the type of agreement based on financial compensation, where fishing rights are exchanged for cash, towards agreements based on joint ventures and enterprises?

Answer

The Council of Ministers gave in September 1995 to the Commission a mandate to negotiate a fisheries agreement with South Africa.

This mandate is very wide and allows both parties to find the best way of promoting their fisheries relations through a fisheries agreement.

Up to now, the negotiations have not yet begun but during the exploratory talks the Commission had with the South African fisheries authorities, it proposed a type of fisheries agreement which is a combination of classical licences and joint operations.

The negotiations will start on 8 April in Brussels.

Question No 58 by Birgitta Ahlqvist (H−0323/98)

Subject: Precedence of EC‐law over national legislation

In Sweden, since 1992, the Jåmo, the official institution responsible for monitoring the implementation of laws on gender equality, has been able to check collective agreements between trade unions and employers' associations.

Since 1994, the law on equal pay for equal work has been improved to cover 'equivalent' work as well.

The Jåmo now has a problem with the trade unions, since this law is not being implemented correctly, and the trade unions feel that they, and not the Jåmo, should be monitoring implementation of the law. On the other hand, Jåmo affirms that the obligation to adapt Swedish law to existing European legislation, in particular Article 141 (formerly Article 119) and derived therefrom, is absolute.

What is the Commission's view?

Answer

The Community law of equal treatment between men and women as regards pay has been applied to Sweden before accession since the entry into force of the EEA Agreement on 1 January 1994. (Art. 119 of the Treaty and Directive 75/117/EEC).

Pursuant to Article 189 of the EC Treaty, Swedish authorities may choose to authorize trade unions to implement a Community law directive through collective agreement and to check by themselves whether they are correctly applied. However, the same provision binds Sweden as to the result to be achieved by the Directive.

Article 4 of the said Directive requires Member States to take the necessary measures to ensure that provisions appearing in collective agreements which are contrary to the principle of equal pay shall be, or may be declared, null and void or may be amended.

This means that Sweden would be held responsible in case of an improper implementation by trade unions of any obligation arising out of that Directive.

Question No 59 by Hugh McMahon (H−0341/98)

Subject: Information and consultation at national level

Can the Commission inform Parliament of its position with regard to UNICE's decision not to enter into social dialogue negotiations on information and consultation at national level?

Does the Commission agree that the decision of several of UNICE's national federations to oppose negotiation presents a serious setback for social dialogue, and does it now consider it necessary to bring forward a legislative proposal on this issue?

Answer

The Commission very much regrets that UNICE has decided, at this time, not to enter into negotiations on the issue of information and consultation within the national framework, particularly since the ETUC and CEEP have expressed their willingness to conclude an agreement at European level on this subject.

The Commission believes that it is absolutely necessary for the Community to meet the legitimate concerns of the European citizens on the adequacy of the rules in force in the Member States in this field.

These concerns were reflected in the consternation caused by the Renault‐Vilvorde case last year. This was just one concrete example of the need to rethink policies and rules in the field of information and consultation of workers, which have proven, at least on that occasion, not to be sufficient.

Throughout the consultation procedures carried out since June last year, the Commission has always firmly expressed its view that this issue should preferably be dealt with, at European level, by the social partners themselves.

In fact it is difficult to find a more appropriate subject on which to use the contractual arrangements provided for in the Social Protocol, as opposed to the traditional legislative route.

It touches some of the more fundamental questions currently on the table of the social partners, at all levels, in relation to the fights against unemployment, work organization, adaptation to change, and the competitiveness of the European industry.

However, in the absence of a negotiation, it is the Commission's intention to present shortly with a proposal for a Directive on information and consultation, in order to ensure that basic rules for information and consultation of workers are established throughout the Community.

More generally, this unfortunate decision from UNICE gives an extremely bad signal about the European level social dialogue, at a time when the social partners are asked to provide a decisive contribution to pursuing some of the main policies and priorities, notably in relation to the fight against unemployment.

The Commission will meet the social partners later this week in order to prepare for the mini‐summit on social dialogue which will be hosted in early May. This mini‐summit will analyze the present situation of the social dialogue at European level, and examine how the social partners intend to fulfil the expectations created by the conclusions of the Luxembourg Jobs Summit.

Question No 60 by Mihail Papayannakis (H−0356/98)

Subject: Reform of human resources programmes

All the Community Support Frameworks are being reformed during the 1994‐1999 period so as to take into account progress made in development at both national and Community levels and to enable all the funds to taken up.

Can the Commission say what progress has been made in reforming the human resources programmes in Greece, how much money remains unused and what prospects exist for using it?

Answer

In the Greek CSF, it was foreseen that operational programmes would be evaluated at mid‐term and reviewed accordingly, in order to take account of current policy developments at national and Community level, experience acquired in the meantime, as well as progress in implementation. The mid‐term evaluation of the CSF and the individual programmes have been delayed. Evaluation reports from independent evaluators are expected in the next few months.

In the case of the human resources programmes, the revision exercise started last October and has not yet been finalized. The early completion of the exercise is important for both the Commission and the national authorities. It is hoped that the reprogramming of the human resources programmes, which constitute an essential tool in the implementation of the European employment strategy, will be concluded before June. All possibilities to reprogramme action within operational programmes – that is, without changing the initial financial allocations between operational programmes will be examined. Transfer of appropriations from programme to programme will be considered only where internal reprogramming cannot ensure full use of credits. Meetings of the operational programmes' Monitoring Committees have been planned in April, May and June in order for the revised programmes to be adopted.

At this point in time, the ESF appropriations not yet paid to Greece amount to approximately ECU 1770m (i.e., total ESF budget in the CSF minus payments already made). Despite the delay in the implementation of the ESF interventions in Greece up to now, the Commission is convinced that ESF credits can and must be fully used, given, inter alia, their relatively low percentage in the CSF and the considerable need for human resources investment.

The full and effective use of credits by the end of the programming period depends on the successful revision of the operational programmes now underway.

Question No 61 by Christine Oddy (H−0367/98)

Subject: Harmonization of the minimum wage

Now that the United Kingdom is introducing legislation to implement a minimum wage, what steps will the Commission take to encourage harmonization of the minimum wage throughout Europe?

What significance will the single currency have for the minimum wage and for wage‐bargaining across Europe?

Answer

Wage bargaining is not within the competence of the Commission, and it should be underlined that Article 2(6) of the Agreement on social policy clearly indicates that its provisions shall not apply to pay.

The Commission has no intention to propose measures to harmonize rules on or levels of minimum wages in the Community.

Wage bargaining is a matter mainly for the social partners. As a general observation one can say that the recent wage agreements in many Member States seem to take into account the requirements of the convergence criteria to keep inflation at the current relatively low level.

It is true that comparisons of wages between Member States may, at least in principle, be easier after the introduction of the single currency. However, one should not forget that any comparisons will also have to take into account the varying productivity and price levels and the diverse fiscal and social security systems in different Member States. In other words, simplistic nominal comparisons should be avoided. In the short term the Commission cannot see any reasons to change policies in the social field which would be directly linked to the introduction of the single currency.

On the contrary, efforts should be continued to strengthen the European social model which is based on a healthy and competitive economy. The Commission thinks the single currency can help in this respect.

Question No 62 by Brian Crowley (H−0370/98)

Subject: Reform of the Social Fund

Given the need to ensure that young people are fully trained in the use of new technologies and communications systems as they enter the labour force, can the Commission indicate if priority will be given under new Social Fund legislation to support for the special skills courses being offered to young people who have completed their formal education, such as the PLC courses in Ireland and will it actively liaise with the secondary education sector to ensure that young people are offered training in these skills between the end of their formal education and their entry into the labour force?

Answer

The proposed new Regulation governing the European Social Fund (ESF) sets out a policy framework for the Member States. This includes five important policy areas, of which two are directly relevant here:

1. The development of active labour market policies to support the occupational integration of young people; and

2. The development of education and training systems as part of lifelong learning policy to enhance and sustain employability and integration into the labour market.

It is also provided that the plans submitted by the Member States to draw down ESF funding must also reflect their National Plans for Employment under the Employment Guidelines. The first Guidelines already adopted for 1998 include – as part of the ‚Employability’ pillar – two strong guidelines of relevance here:

1. With a view to easing the transition from school to work, the quality of school systems should be improved to ensure they equip young people with greater ability to adapt to technological and economic changes and with skills relevant to the labour market, where appropriate through apprenticeship training.

2. As part of the development of preventive and employability‐oriented strategies to tackle youth unemployment, every unemployed young person should be offered a new start in form of training, retraining, work practice, a job or other employability measure before reaching six months of unemployment.

The Commission believes that the bringing together of the European Employment Strategy and the reformed ESF will give a strong boost for new policies and measures to meet the needs identified by the honourable Member. It should, however, be recalled that the design and delivery of specific measures under the European Social Fund is a matter for each Member State to decide within the overall strategy agreed with the Commission.

Question No 65 by John Cushnahan (H−0274/98)

Subject: Effects of abolition of duty free sales

At a time when many Member States within the Union are having to make difficult economic and social policy decisions to qualify for EMU, and with the Community committed to further expansion under the Agenda 2000 programme, would the Commission confirm that sufficient Community funds will be immediately available to alleviate the adverse social and economic consequences of abolition of tax and duty free sales within the Union, without placing a further burden on Member States' finances?

Answer

The Commission would like to emphasize that Member States are responsible for deciding what action they intend to take in order to solve specific problems.

As far as the Community Structural Funds are concerned, they are implemented through a partnership framework.

The Commission would however like to point out that EC Structural Funds are not intended to compensate for the general consequences of changes in the tax system. Their aim is to take action in favour of regions or social groups affected by structural problems, in accordance with Articles 130A and 130E of the EC Treaty.

Question No 66 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H−0177/98)

Subject: Taxation

Sweden and Finland are currently embroiled in a dispute over where certain persons are required to pay tax. The reason for the dispute is Directive 83/182/EEC(5) .

Will the Commission state whether a person automatically becomes liable to pay tax in a Member State other than that of which he is a national or a resident if he spends more than 185 nights there? Does this also apply if the person concerned is visiting friends or summer residences? Do any court judgments exist in this area indicating how the 185night rule is to be interpreted?

Answer

As the Commission outlined in its answer to a similar question by the Member(6) , the “185 day’ rule is laid down in Article 7(1) of Directive 83/182/EEC, which gives a definition of normal residence.

’"Normal residence" means the place where a person usually lives, that is for at least 185 days in each calendar year, because of personal or occupational ties, or in the case of a person with no occupational ties, because of personal ties which show close links between that person and the place where he is living.’

With regard to the above concept, which is not the same as a person"s nationality, the Court of Justice of the European Communities ruled, in response to a preliminary question similar to the Member"s(7) , that normal residence within the meaning of Article 7(1) of the Directive, means the permanent centre of the person"s interests. The place must be determined on the basis of all the criteria contained in the provision and all the other pertinent facts. We can therefore deduce from this that spending 185 days per year in another Member State, without any other elements to show that the permanent centre of the person"s interests has been moved, is insufficient to prove a change of normal residence, with all the resulting consequences from the fiscal point of view.

The consequences can be summarized as follows:

Registration tax (“Autovero ’ in Finland) is payable, in theory, in the person"s country of normal residence;

However, road fund tax (’moottoriajoneuvovero/motorfordonsskatt ’ in Finland) may also be payable, in accordance with Article 9 of the Directive, in the Member State of temporary importation, if the admissible period is exceeded (generally six months in every twelve, in accordance with Article 3 of the Directive) with the authorization of the Member State in question, which has a power of discretion in this respect.

Question No 67 by Elly Plooij‐van Gorsel (H−0290/98)

Subject: Compliance with European public tendering rules

The Netherlands Broadcasting Production Company (Nederlandse Omroepproduktie Bedrijf – NOB) is the biggest independent organization providing technical facilities for audiovisual productions in Europe. A privatization process has been under way since 1995. The NOB nevertheless continues to be entrusted with the government‐imposed task of providing transmission facilities for national public broadcasting (radio and TV).

Can the Commission determine whether the NOB is subject to European public tendering rules?

If so, is the Commission aware that the NOB regularly procures equipment (e.g. purchase of cameras) without issuing invitations to tender? What action will the Commission take in this connection?

Answer

If ‚Nederlandse Omroepproduktie Bedrijf ’ (Netherlands Broadcasting Production Company – NOB) was a ‚contracting authority’ or more specifically a ‚body governed by public law’, as defined in EC ‚public contracts’ directives, it would be subject to European public tendering rules.

A body governed by public law means any body:

‐ established for the specific purpose of meeting needs in the general interest, not having an industrial or commercial character;

‐ which has a legal personality;

‐ which is financed, for the most part, by the state, regional or local authorities, or other bodies governed by public law, or subject to management supervision by those bodies, and having an administrative, managerial or supervisory board, more than half of whose members are appointed by the state, regional or local authorities or by other bodies governed by public law.

These conditions are cumulative. If one of the three conditions is not fulfilled, the body in question is not a ‚body governed by public law’.

At the present time, on the basis of the information provided by the Member and its own information, the Commission is unable to determine whether NOB meets the first and third conditions.

With regard to the first condition, the fact that ‚Nederlandse Omroepproduktie Bredrijf ’ continues to be officially entrusted with the task of national public radio and TV broadcasting does not automatically mean it was created for the specific purpose of meeting needs in the general interest, which do not have an industrial or commercial character.

As far as the third condition is concerned, the Commission does not have enough information to be able to determine whether or not ‚Nederlandse Omroepproduktie Bredrijf ’ fulfils that condition. In order to decide whether it meets that condition, the Commission requires information about:

‐ whether ‚Nederlandse Omroepproduktie Bredrijf ’ is financed by the state, regional or local authorities or other bodies governed by public law;

‐ whether ‚Nederlandse Omroepproduktie Bredrijf ’ is subject to management supervision by the state, regional or local authorities or other bodies governed by public law;

‐ the composition of the administrative, managerial or supervisory board of ‚Nederlandse Omroepproduktie Bredrijf ’ (whether more than half of its members are appointed by the state, regional or local authorities or by other bodies governed by public law).

In order to answer the above question, the Commission will contact the Member State to enquire about the facts to which the Member refers. The Commission will advise the Member of the results of its investigations.

Question No 68 by Angela Billingham (H−0295/98)

Subject: British ski instructors in the French Alps

Could the Commission inform me whether any progress is being made by the Franco‐British Working Party which was established to examine the issue of training systems for ski instructors in France and Britain and, in particular, to examine whether there is a substantial difference between the BASI Grade 1 Diploma held by British Instructors and the French Brevet D'Etat held by the French Instructors?

What role is the European Commission, as an external organization, playing in this bilateral working party to ensure neutrality?

After the Working Party has completed its task, what action does the European Commission intend to take to ensure that British BASI Grade 1 ski instructors are no longer prevented from teaching on the French Alps?

Answer

The Commission has already made great efforts to initiate a bilateral dialogue between the BASI (British Association of Ski Instructors) and the French authorities. The aim is to encourage direct discussions on the conditions governing the recognition of the ‚BASI Grade 1’ Diploma in France. A first meeting, chaired by the Commission, took place in Brussels at the end of 1997 between BASI and the French competent authorities. After the constructive meeting and at the Commission's initiative, it was decided to constitute a Franco‐British working group to examine the question of the ‚training systems in place in the two countries’. One of the principle tasks of the working groups is to answer the following questions: ’Is there a substantial difference between the BASI Grade 1 diploma and the French Brevet d'état du premier degré d'éducateur sportif’ ? Conclusions should be ready, at the latest, by Spring 1998

It was also decided to organize direct, immediate contacts between France and the BASI to solve the problems as soon as they occur (by way of fax, telephone and direct contact between those in charge). Finally it was agreed to inform systematically the Commission about the results of the working group.

A first bilateral meeting took place recently in Paris between BASI and the French authorities. It will be followed by other contacts where all the practical problems and obstacles will be discussed. The honourable Member will easily understand that the Commission cannot, before this group has given its conclusions, prejudge what its reaction will be. The Commission can only ensure that in all cases it will carefully check the correct implementation of Community law in the Alps. This means that the national authorities will be allowed to impose aptitude tests only in cases of significant differences in the levels of qualifications. It is precisely one of the main tasks of the bilateral group to determine if these substantial differences exist.

Question No 69 by Jan Andersson (H−0302/98)

Subject: Commission proposal for a Council Directive creating a mechanism whereby the Commission can intervene in order to remove certain obstacles to trade

There is concern in a number of Member States that the creation of a mechanism providing for intervention to remove certain obstacles to trade may adversely affect trade union rights. The proposal apparently also came in for a good deal of criticism when it was examined by the Council.

Will the Commission explain what account was taken of trade union rights, in particular as regards their legal status in relation to the legal status of the freedom of movement, and what contact it had with the two sides of industry, when drawing up the proposal?

Answer

First of all, the Commission would like to stress that it considers the proposal for a regulation referred to by the Member, which introduces a mechanism of rapid intervention by the Commission in response to certain serious types of barrier to the free movement of goods, to be the most appropriate means of fulfilling the specific mandate given to it by the Amsterdam European Council. That mandate, it is important to remember, asked the Commission to submit proposals to increase the speed and effectiveness of the present procedures, and of course, to provide added value.

When drafting the proposal, the Commission tried to avoid any conflict between trade union rights and the implementation of the intervention mechanism. In fact, the proposal for a regulation states clearly and precisely that the intervention mechanism will not affect the exercising of fundamental rights recognized by national laws. The implementation of the intervention mechanism therefore does not aim to or enable the Commission to interfere in the social dialogue at a national level. The Commission thus did not consider it necessary to consult the social partners when drafting the proposal.

Question No 70 by Hubert Pirker (H−0315/98)

Subject: Abuse of freedom to provide services

There have been repeated cases in Austria of firms from Member States posting third country nationals to Austria as cheap labour even though their connection with the firm in question is purely fictitious.

What measures does the Commission propose to take to put an end to this abuse, which constitutes a distortion of competition?

Does the obligation to supply documentation constitute an infringement of the freedom to provide services?

Answer

Posting of workings in the framework of cross‐border provision of services represents a phenomenon of increasing importance in the internal market.

The question which has been raised concerns two different issues:

The first relates to minimum rate of pay and covers all workers regardless of their nationality:

The prohibition to exploit workers affects all workers regardless of whether they are Community or third country nationals. This also affects undertakings which otherwise suffer from a distortion of competition. For these reasons, the European Parliament and the Council adopted on 16 December 1996(8) a directive concerning the posting of workers in the framework of service provision. The directive allows a Member State to whom workers are posted to require the respect of a minimum rate of pay as well as of other minimum working conditions. It is now up to the Member States to implement the directive by 16 December 1999 in accordance with Community law.

The second issue relates to the granting of visas, residence and work permits to third country nationals:

Third country nationals do not benefit from the principle of free movement of workers this does not prevent their posting in the framework of free provision of services. An undertaking established in the Community and wanting to exercise its right to provide services is entitled to post its own worker to another Member State. The worker must be in possession of a residence and work permit of his country of residence.

At present it is unclear to which extent Member States can still impose visa, residence and work permit requirements on such posted workers. The Court of Justice stated, in a ruling of 1994(9) , that requiring a work permit is incompatible with Community law. However, the judgment only referred to ‚lawfully and habitually employees’ workers and left many other issues open. Therefore, the report of the high level panel on free movement of persons, known as the ‚Veil report’, recommended to the Commission to undertake action at Community level to solve this problem. The Commission is currently examining the possibility of a Community initiative. The Commission should reach a decision on this matter in the summer.

Question No 71 by Ian White (H−0316/98)

Subject: Voting rights of EU citizens

Is it for the Commission to ensure that EU citizens living throughout the Union have voting rights at local, regional, national and European elections?

Answer

Article 8B of the EC Treaty states that any citizen residing in a Member State of which he is not a national has the right to vote and to stand for election in local and European Parliament elections.

With a view to the implementation of these rights, in 1993 and 1994, the Council unanimously adopted two directives containing detailed provisions to be implemented by the Member States, and in 1996, the Council adopted a directive adding the local authorities of the three new Member States.

These new rights were implemented in June 1994 with regard to the European elections in twelve Member States, and in the new Member States, in 1995 in Sweden and in 1996 in Austria and Finland.

On the same subject, the Commission would like to draw the attention of the Members of this House to its report to the European Parliament and to the Council on the right to vote and to stand for election to the European Parliament of 7 January 1998, and on its report on citizenship of the Union of 27 May 1997.

With regard to local elections, all the Member States have adopted implementing measures, except Belgium and France. The Commission is bringing actions against these two Member States in accordance with Article 169 of the EC Treaty. With regard to Belgium, proceedings have reached the stage of referral to the European Court of Justice.

Please note also that the directives do not seek to harmonize national electoral systems.

Finally, the Commission wishes to point out that the right to vote and to stand for election in regional and national elections is not laid down in EC law.

Question No 72 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0335/98)

Subject: No‐claims bonus in compulsory insurance

In some Member States, including Greece, the legislation concerning compulsory third‐party liability insurance for motorists provides for a system whereby premiums may be reduced or increased (no‐claims bonus). In Greece, consumers who have taken out policies with insurance companies in good faith enjoy this benefit and any change to the system could result in a significant increase in the average premium since, according to statistics produced by the Union of Insurance Companies, around 70 % of policy‐holders have lower premiums because of the no‐claims bonus system.

What is the Commission's view of the system that applies in Greece? Does it consider it to be contrary to the third directives? What measures will it take to prevent an increase in premiums, particularly for those drivers who have proved to be most sensible and careful on the roads?

Answer

Within the context of the creation of the internal insurance market, Directive 92/49/EEC (third non‐life insurance directive) which is applicable to car insurance, introduced freedom of pricing and the removal of any prior or systematic control of prices and policies. In accordance with the above directive, in 1996, Greek legislation abolished the standard no‐claim bonus system, which was compulsory for all insurers operating in Greece, and which did not allow insurance companies in the car insurance sector to define their own premiums. Now insurers can define their own systems of decreasing or increasing premiums on the basis of their own criteria.

1. Insurance companies are therefore free to apply the no‐claims bonus system of their choice, in order to reward careful drivers.

2. Inasmuch as Directive 92/49/EEC specifically prohibited pricing controls, the abolition of the no‐claim bonus system containing pricing elements brought Greek legislation into line with EC insurance law.

3. The abolition of a compulsory no‐claim bonus system does not automatically lead to an increase in premiums paid by careful drivers. Furthermore, the Commission is not responsible for the definition of premiums by insurance companies. In general, competition between insurers operating in the same market encourages them to offer advantageous conditions to careful drivers. If an increase in premiums is observed in the Greek market, it will be the result of a decision by the Greek supervisory authority (supervisory authority of the Greek Department of Trade), to increase the financial stability of the insurance companies in question, and enable them to fulfil their contractual obligations.

Question No 73 by James Fitzsimons (H−0353/98)

Subject: Compensation for Irish companies and workers affected by blockades and other tactics in Britain discriminating against Irish products

The Council has stated in response to my earlier question on compensation for Irish companies and workers affected by blockades in Britain (No H0982/97) that 'it is the responsibility of the Commission to ensure the free movement of goods within the European Union'.

Last December the Court of Justice reprimanded France for failing to ensure that imports from Spain and Belgium could get through blockades in France thereby failing to fulfil obligations under European Union law.

Will the Commission now state what it is doing to safeguard the rights of Irish exporters and to ensure immediate compensation is paid to Irish companies, farmers and workers affected by the blockading of British ports and subsequent actions in supermarkets?

Answer

As the Member says, the Court of Justice ruled(10) that France had violated Article 30 of the Treaty, in accordance with Article 5 of the Treaty, by failing to adopt adequate measures to ensure the free movement of fruit and vegetables in France. For the first time, therefore, the Court of Justice considered a Member State"s failure to take action against the activities of private individuals was a government measure, which was incompatible with Article 30.

It is therefore the responsibility of national courts(11) to take into consideration the conclusions of the above‐mentioned ruling, and to assess the rights of individuals who bring actions before such courts. In fact, as the Member says, individuals may obtain compensation for losses sustained due to barriers incompatible with Article 30 of the Treaty.

The Commission has proposed a Council Regulation(12) , based on Article 235, creating an intervention mechanism to enable the Commission to adopt decisions asking Member States to take appropriate action to remove certain types of barriers to trade. The mechanism will enable injured parties to ensure that national courts implement the Commission"s decisions very quickly. Thus they will be able, by means of national legal remedies and means of redress, to ensure the removal of the barrier to trade that is causing a loss, or where appropriate, to obtain compensation. This will be beneficial to individuals in terms of speed and efficiency. Until that regulation comes into force, the present compensation procedures to which the Member refers will remain applicable.

Question No 74 by Charlotte Cederschiöld (H−0271/98)

Subject: Hunting of predatory animals

The Union is meant to safeguard the diversity of animal life, but not to put people's lives at risk in the process. In the region north of Lake Siljan in Dalarna, Sweden, it is possible to observe how the countryside is becoming increasingly wild. As only a few people live in the villages, predatory animals are venturing nearer and nearer. Many locals say that numbers of roe deer have plummeted because they are being eaten by lynx. Bears are becoming more numerous and getting increasingly close to settlements. Recently, a couple taking a walk through the forest was attacked by an illtempered bear in Rättvik. As the countryside is not being taken care of as it used to be and not being kept open, it is changing and becoming wilder. Further north, farmers are complaining about wolves. Since livestock is being killed by lynx and children are encountering bears outside their homes, is it not time to consider giving locals a greater say in decisions concerning predatory animals?

Is the Union able to take responsibility for ensuring that the rules on the conservation of wild animals do not become inappropriate and are not overtaken by reality, thus exposing people to danger or significant nuisance? Should such matters not be governed to a greater extent by subsidiarity and the relevant decisions be taken more at the local level?

Answer

According to the Habitats Directive 92/43/EEC(13) such big predatory mammals existing in the Community such as the brown bear, wolf (with the exception of some populations) and lynx are strictly protected. Among other things, all forms of deliberate capture an killing of these species in the wild are prohibited.

Even when it has been necessary to provide such a protection regime for these species, which in many parts of the Community have been exterminated a long time ago, the Commission is well aware that these species do occasionally prey on domestic animals and sometimes may even pose a threat to human safety. This situation is adequately provided for in Article 16 of the Habitats Directive by making it possible to derogate from the strict protection provisions, when there is no satisfactory alternative, for specific reasons such as in the interest of public safety and to prevent serious damage to livestock.

These provisions of the Habitats Directive when carried over to the national laws provide, whilst respecting the principle of subsidiarity as mentioned by the Honourable Member, sufficient possibilities to overall regulation of the populations of big predatory mammals by hunting as well as dealing with more immediate threats to human beings and livestock by removing particular individuals of these species. The relevant decisions have to be taken at a national level.

Question No 75 by Peter Truscott (H−0272/98)

Subject: CAP funding

Commissioner Fischler stated in his response E−4091/97 to my previous question that he is unable to provide a breakdown of CAP funding by areas of UK counties. If the Commission cannot provide such statistics, how can such expenditure by monitored and fraud be prevented?

Answer

With regard to EAGGF‐guarantee expenditure the Commission is informed of the total CAP‐funding for each measure in each Member State. It cannot verify the expenditure by and in every region or by every peripheral governmental department. It is the Member State which must ensure by appropriate control structures that expenditure is carried out in conformity with Community rules. Through the reform of the clearance of accounts procedure decided in 1995, stringent rules have been established in order to ensure that Member States administrative structure and control procedures are complete, effective and transparent. One of the main elements of the reform is that paying agencies have to be approved before funding with the aim that reasonable assurance is guaranteed that only eligible expenditure will be charged to the fund. Likewise it is only after verification by a separate and independent body (the certifying body) of the existence of effective control that an accreditation as paying agency is given. The certifying body has to conduct its examination according to internationally accepted auditing standards. Accreditation can only be given provided that internal control procedures have operated satisfactorily, condition which has to be verified by an internal control department. Last but not least the reform also required Member States to install a coordination body which acts as sole representative of the Member States before the Commission. The role of the Commission is to verify that the Member States' control procedures operate as provided in the regulations which it does under the annual accounts clearance and during the audit of the compliance of individual measures.

Turning to EAGGF (guidance) expenditure, virtually all payments under EAGGF (guidance) are made either in the context of programmes or under so‐called 'horizontal' measures. Programmes cover specific geographic areas which generally cover entities larger than counties. There is no programme that covers only the county of Hertfordshire mentioned by the Honourable Member in his question 4091/97. Concerning 'horizontal' measures, these are applicable across an entire national territory, including therefore Hertfordshire.

Monitoring as well as verification of expenditure is carried out by the Commission on the basis of declarations submitted by the Member State concerned. The Commission checks these declarations, and within the limits of its staff resources, makes on‐the‐spot checks. This latter exercise is carried out on a selective basis in the various Member States. Once the measures to be the subject of the checks have been identified the Commission requires the Member States to provide all relevant documentation in support of the expenditure claim. This would include names and addresses of the beneficiaries concerned. This documentation is then examined to verify conformity with Community provisions. That exercise, as well as detection of fraud, does not require knowledge by the Commission of expenditure in any particular county. It should be remembered that it is the primary responsibility of Member States to take measures that minimize the incidence of overpayments and avoid fraud.

Question No 76 by Arlene McCarthy (H−0277/98)

Subject: Commission's anti‐dumping investigation into unbleached cotton fabric from China, Egypt, India, Indonesia, Pakistan and Turkey

Now that its inquiry has concluded, would the Commission please briefly explain to Members its findings and the importance of the Community Interest investigation to its recommendations?

The complainant, Eurocoton, estimated that 5 500 out of the 115 000 people employed by EU weavers are in direct competition with imported unbleached cotton fabric. Given the strength of opposition to duties from many sectors of the textiles and related industries and the fact that conservative estimates predict that a far greater 23 000 to 41 000 jobs will be threatened in the European finishing industry alone if duties are imposed, would the Commission not agree that the Community's interests would be ill served by such duties?

Answer

Following an investigation, the Commission has decided to impose provisional measures on imports of unbleached cotton fabrics originating in the PRC, India, Indonesia, Pakistan, Egypt and Turkey, since injurious dumping has taken place and since no compelling reasons have been found against the Community interest not to adopt measures.

Concerning the Community interest aspects of the case, the provisional analysis shows that the Community industry will very likely benefit from the imposition of anti‐dumping measures in the form of increased production, sales, reduction of stocks and regaining of profitability. The benefits to the Community industry from the imposition of anti‐dumping measures will not be impaired by a surge in the imports from other third countries not covered by the investigation, since the majority of these other low‐cost third countries are also subjected to quotas, their price level is above that of the countries concerned and they are too fragmented to constitute a durable and stable alternative source of supply.

The issue of employment has been carefully examined. The overall employment relating to weaving of the product concerned has been estimated at around 40 000 comprising 12 000 in non‐captive weaving. Concerning spinners, who are highly dependent on the Community weavers, employment has been estimated at around 10 000. Concerning finishers, employment has been estimated at around 27 000.

As regards the upstream industry (spinners), given their dependence on the weaving industry, it is likely that any benefits to Community weavers will also positively affect Community spinners.

As far as downstream industry is concerned, namely the finishers (which bleach, dye and print unbleached cotton fabrics), it has been concluded that, given their comparative advantage in terms of quality, know‐how, fashion and market proximity, the risk of accrued competition from third country imports of finished products would probably be limited, especially because such imports would be restricted by quotas. Furthermore, when the above‐mentioned provisional duties were in place, prices of the imported unbleached cotton fabrics did not increase by the full amount of the duty imposed, and those price increases which were observed could be passed on down the commercial chain.

As regards makers‐up (who transform the finished fabrics into made‐up goods, such as shirts or bed linen), it was concluded that any negative effect of anti‐dumping measures would be small, due to the distance of makers‐up from the product concerned in the production chain and the high value added to their products.

Question No 77 by David Thomas (H−0282/98)

Subject: SRM ban

Does the Commission consider that the proposed regionalization of rules relating to SRMs are compatible with the basic free market principles contained in inter alia Articles 2 and 3 of the Treaties and to what extent would such rules provide effective protection of consumers given the impossibility of policing the proposed measures?

Answer

The Commission always ensures that its proposals are designed to achieve the objectives of the Treaty. Its proposals concerning specified risk material are fully compatible with the principles set out in Articles 2 and 3 of the EC Treaty, in particular the common agriculture policy, the attainment of a high level of health protection and the strengthening of consumer protection.

The proposals on specified risk materials contain provisions for control and enforcement. Should the Commission make proposals in the future to allow derogations from the rules, those proposals will contain any specific control requirements which are necessary in order to allow the internal market to function effectively.

Question No 78 by Shaun Spiers (H−0283/98)

Subject: Agricultural spending on biotechnology

The Fourth Framework Programme encompasses several agricultural research projects including “Biotechnology“ and “Agriculture and Fisheries’.

What plans does the Commission have to give support to those who grow organic food and who oppose the growth in biotechnology within agriculture?

Answer

The objectives of the Agriculture and Fisheries (including ago‐industry, food technologies, forestry, aquaculture and rural development) specific programme are to promote and harmonize research in the major European primary production food and non‐food sectors of agriculture, horticulture, forestry, fisheries and aquaculture and its links with the input and processing industries, together with the rural activities, the end user and the consumer.

New markets and products of greater added value have to be developed for raw materials produced by agriculture and forestry which meet the demands and requirements of the end‐users, in particular through the improvement of their quality.

More specifically, research in the agricultural sector must respond to socio‐economic issues of rural communities, to safety, quality and health and environmental implications of new food and non‐food products for which it is clear that there is a demand from the public.

Biotechnology related projects could fit and consequently could be considered as eligible into this research programme.

Research activities dealing with the development of organic farming production systems are also included in the FAIR work programme and are eligible for funding as well.

The project proposals are selected according to a careful assessment procedure, involving an independent evaluation by outside experts, extensive series of consultation with the Commission and discussion with the Programme Committee.

The first criterion the Commission has to consider for research projects is the scientific excellence of the proposals, but also their socio‐economic aspects, their agronomic impact as well as their effect on the development of the concerned agricultural markets. Relevant ethical, legal and social aspects must be taken into account. In particular, Community legislation related to the applications of biotechnology must be fully respected.

Question No 79 by Veronica Hardstaff (H−0289/98)

Subject: Harmonization of the use of pesticides in the EU

What steps is the Commission taking to ensure EU‐wide harmonization of the use of pesticides and the dissemination of data on research on pesticides use to avoid duplication of research and differing standards in different Member States?

Answer

Directive 91/414/EEC concerning the placing of plant protection products on the market provides for the Community framework within which the marketing and use of plant protection products is regulated. It provides for general provisions with regard to the use of plant protection products in accordance with good plant protection practice. Further possible measures with regard to sustainable use of plant protection products are under examination in the Commission. The matter is also addressed in the 5th Environmental Action Programme, and will be discussed in a workshop organized by the Commission from 12 to 14 May 1998. During this workshop the results of certain research activities concerning pesticide use will be discussed.

As far as data on use of pesticides is concerned, only very aggregated sales data are available for all Member States. They do not provide any information on individual active ingredients, doses used, crops treated, etc.

However, certain Member States, (Netherlands, Sweden, UK) carry out regular surveys on pesticide use in agriculture, which are more detailed and include such information.

Because of the usefulness of such detailed pesticide usage information, the Commission (Eurostat) has drawn up guidelines for the collection of pesticide usage data, with the intention of starting up regular surveys in Member States. It should be ensured that in the near future all Member States carry out systematical surveys on the basis of these guidelines, subject of course to clearance of the financial implications of organizing such surveys.

Question No 80 by Klaus Lukas (H−0292/98)

Subject: Assistance scheme for border countries in the context of eastward enlargement

Because of the different competitive situations of the CEECs and the EU Member States, massive adjustments are needed on both sides in the context of enlargement to the east.

What is your attitude to the call to set up a special 'Assistance scheme for border countries' for the benefit of EU regions particularly affected by eastward enlargement?

Answer

Although enlargement towards central and eastern Europe and the resulting opening of markets will make some changes necessary, the Commission considers that the enlargement process and the abolition of existing barriers at the borders with the CEECs will stimulate economic activity considerably. It will create hitherto unknown development prospects in those regions.

As far as EC financial aid is concerned, as well as specific reforms enabling the Member States to take care of their own specific needs, the Commission is planning to renew a Community initiative with a section relating to cross‐border cooperation, during the next Structural Funds programming period. The initiative will also be an appropriate means of responding to the possible need for structural adaptation in the regions involved, further to enlargement.

Question No 81 by Edgar Schiedermeier (H−0293/98)

Subject: Effluent treatment in Brussels

How is waste water treated in the city of Brussels? Is it true that none of the planned plant is working properly yet?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the situation of waste water treatment and discharge in Brussels.

The Belgian region of Brussels with its ‚19 communes’ does have a sewage collection system serving most of the agglomeration, but no waste water treatment plant. The collected waste water is discharged into the river Senne without treatment.

Existing Community legislation, i.e. the Urban Waste Water Treatment Directive 91/271/EEC(14) , obliges Member States to provide all agglomerations above 2000 p.e.
*with waste water collection systems and
*with waste water treatment plant
(p.e. = population equivalent, a widely used measuring unit equaling the biodegradable daily pollution of one person).
The deadlines for meeting these objectives are 31.12.1998, 31.12.2000 and 31.12.2005, depending on the size of the agglomeration and the character of the receiving water.
As for Brussels, the deadline is 31.12.1998.

The plans by the Brussels region for waste water treatment foresee two waste water treatment plants for Brussels, a southern plan (treating about one third of the population's waste water) and a northern plant (treating about two thirds). The southern plant is under construction and will be operational in 1999, the northern plant will, according to the information provided by the Belgian authorities, be operational in 2003 or 2004.

Thus the European Commission does not consider that Belgium is complying with its obligations under Article Urban Waste Water Treatment Directive 91/271/EEC, and in particular is not conforming with Article 17 for non‐conformity of the implementation programme. It has therefore, in its role as Guardian of the Treaty, taken the necessary steps towards an infringement procedure under Article 169 of the EC Treaty.

Question No 82 by Sören Wibe (H−0306/98)

Subject: Future regional aid – Objective 6

The UK Presidency has informed the Commission that it would like to see the provisions governing aid to sparsely populated areas (present Objective 6) extended to cover parts of Great Britain and the whole of Northern Ireland.

As the Commission has no plans for additional appropriations, this might, for example, mean that the inland regions of northern Sweden will face much tougher competition for existing funds.

Is the Commission working along the lines of these proposals, i.e. is it planning to extend the geographical area covered by the aid paid to areas with low population density (present Objective 6) without increasing the appropriation? Will the criterion 'Arctic or sub‐Arctic climate' still exist in the new aid programmes?

Answer

In compliance with the communication “Agenda 2000: For a Stronger and Wider Union’ the Commission has proposed, in a draft regulation implementing general provisions relating to the Structural Funds, that during the period 2000‐2006, the only regions assimilated with Objective 1 regions will be those at present eligible under Objective 6, and the most remote regions, as defined in the Treaty, even if their per capita GDP exceeds 75 % of the EC average.

“Arctic climate’ is not being used as a criterion to determine the list of regions eligible under Objective 6 during the present programming period, nor will it be taken into consideration in the definition of the regions eligible under Objective 1 during the next programming period.

Question No 83 by Outi Ojala (H−0307/98)

Subject: Year against violence against women

What preparations has the Commission made for implementing the proposal put forward by the European Parliament on 16.09. 1997 that 1999 should be declared European Year against Violence against Women?

Violence against women is a problem in all EU Member States. Every year it results in hundreds of women being killed or disabled, to say nothing of the mental suffering involved. A European Year would be an effective way of breaking down the wall of silence surrounding violence within the family and bringing the issue out into the open so that something can really be done about it.

Answer

The Commission agrees with Mrs Ojala on the great need to join forces in combating violence against women, and that a first step must be to break down the wall of silence surrounding the issue of violence against women.

The Commission is already working on a number of initiatives, some designed to raise people's awareness and knowledge concerning violence against women, others to support specific projects relating to the prevention of violence against women.

One project supported by the Commission is the recently established centre for the monitoring of violence against women. This is an important step towards increasing knowledge and spreading information concerning violence against women.

This project has been made possible my means of assistance under the DAPHNE initiative.

Other ways in which the Commission has tried to increase awareness of violence against women is the action programme on equal treatment for women and men and its communication of 1996 on trafficking in women for the purposes of sexual exploitation. This will be followed up with a new communication in the next few months.

The Commission is also drawing up a communication on measures to combat violence against women, adolescents and children.

This communication, scheduled for adoption by the Commission in April, will propose a legal basis for a new multiannual programme.

These initiatives are important steps in the drive against violence against women but a great deal more will be needed before a positive change in the situation can be brought about.

The Commission greatly appreciates Parliament's proposal that 1999 should be declared European Year against Violence against Women.

The Commission is currently working on an initiative for 1999 entailing two major European measures to combat violence against women.

The first will take the form of a large‐scale European Conference and exhibition to be held on 8 March, International Women's Day. This event will be organized jointly with the European Parliament.
