The Commission hopes that, thanks to this initiative, Women's Day in Europe in 1999 will be marked by a large number of seminars and similar activities on the topic of violence against women.

The other activity planned by the Commission is a closing conference at the end of 1999.

During the period between the two conferences, the Commission hopes that the Member States will organize a number of national activities, seminars and similar events on the topic of violence against women.

The Commission earnestly hopes that this combination of European activities and activities within the Member States will serve to give the issue of violence against women the prominence it deserves.

However, it is clear that any efforts to combat violence against women will only yield decisive results if appropriate financial resources are made available.

The Commission hopes that Parliament will demonstrate how seriously it takes this drive against violence by also ensuring the availability of the financial resources needed to achieve shared objectives.

To build up expectations which cannot be satisfied would be intolerable.

The Commission must therefore stress that if 1999 is to be made the year in which the issue of violence against women comes out into the open once and for all, then together it must be ensured that the instruments and financial resources needed to complete this important task are available.

Question No 84 by Anna Karamanou (H−0309/98)

Subject: Decrease in Official Development Assistance – increase in infant mortality

According to a recent OECD report, Official Development Assistance (ODA) to developing countries fell in 1996 to 0.25 % – the lowest in its history – producing a dramatic impact on infant mortality which has reached 12 million deaths a year. If the current rates of decline continue, ODA will have disappeared by 2015, instead of increasing to 0.7 % of GDP as decided in the UN.

What is the Commission's response to the decline in ODA and how will it intervene to implement the commitment to provide 0.7 % of GDP for the developing countries and put an end to the outrage of children dying of starvation and disease?

Answer

1. Like the Member, the Commission is alarmed at the significant decrease in Official Development Assistance (ODA) between 1992 and 1996.

The Commission has observed in fact that the Official Development Assistance provided by DAC countries decreased by 16 % in real terms and the proportion of ODA in the GDP of the DAC countries decreased from 0.33 % to 0.25 %.

The Commission notes, however, that the decrease can mainly be attributed to a reduction in the contributions of the United States and Japan. It is due to a much smaller extent to the decrease in the contribution of the EC, which is still the largest (56 % of total assistance compared with 17 % from the US) and the most generous donor(0.4 % of GDP compared with 0.21 % from the US and 0.28 % from Japan).

The Commission itself does not have any power of decision with regard to the level of ODA and the commitment to provide 0.7 % of GDP as assistance for the developing countries.

2. At present, the Commission does not have any reliable figures or information showing any recent and significant rise in infant and child mortality on an international level that could be linked with the decrease in Official Development Assistance.

A significant increase in infant and child mortality has in fact been observed in a group of countries where armed conflict, serious social or economic difficulties and/or HIV/AIDS infection are prevalent.

The Commission notes that the Community and the Member States are giving those countries considerable support.

Question No 85 by Felipe Camisón Asensio (H−0312/98)

Subject: The welfare of farm animals

With a view to the imminent reform of the common agricultural policy (CAP), does the Commission intend to include measures regarding animal welfare?

Answer

In Agenda 2000 the Commission has proposed to allocate more budgetary resources to the agri‐environmental measures in the framework of a reorganized rural development policy. This policy itself is likely to have favourable repercussions for animal welfare, e.g. moves towards the encouragement of more extensive systems of livestock rearing.

It must not be overlooked that the Community has already a considerable body of legislation designed to protect and to improve the welfare of farm livestock. This legislation is kept under continuous examination and modified and expanded, where necessary, to meet the challenges of both economic developments and scientific progress.

The Commission will take further initiatives in this sector in accordance with the obligations of the Treaties.

Question No 86 by Richard Howitt (H−0322/98)

Subject: Voluntary organizations and access to Structural Funds

Given that, according to the Commission's communication of 4 June 1997 (COM(97)0241 final), between 2 and 4.2 per cent of jobs in the four Member States surveyed is created by voluntary organizations and that the combined expenditure of these countries ECU 127 billion per year, resulting in between 2 and 4.8 per cent of GNP being attributed by these organizations, what practical measures does the Commission intend to take to ensure that these organizations are able to maximize their potential by securing easier access to European Structural Funds in the next programming period, and does it intend to use the valuable experience gained at this level by increasing consultation with all partners?

Answer

For the next programming period, the Commission has proposed, firstly, enlarging the composition of the Partnership to include the regional and local authorities, the economic and social partners, as well as other competent organizations: within this latter category, organizations representing the voluntary sector might be considered; secondly, that it will be the responsibility of the Member States to ensure the participation of all the partners at all stages of the programming process.

It should however be stressed that these proposals have yet to be discussed by Parliament and the Council.

The voluntary sector's possible contribution to actions co‐financed by the Structural Funds is already envisaged under datasheet No 7 ‚national part‐financing in kind’ – one of the 22 datasheets covering the most problematic areas of expenditure eligibility under the funds, which were adopted by the Commission on 23 April 1997.

The Commission will carry out an appraisal, before mid−1999, of the application of all 22 datasheets by the Member States to determine which might be usefully carried over into and given legal status in the new programming period.

Question No 87 by Ioannis Theonas (H−0324/98)

Subject: Illegal operation of Elbiex factory in Ioannina region of Greece

Two years after the Commission's answer, the factory continues its criminal operations unhindered, despite the protests of the thousands of inhabitants of the 53 villages in the Ioannina basin, exposing the population of the area to enormous health hazards.

What measures will the Commission take to close down the Elbiex factory immediately, to carry out the necessary works to complete the water supply and sewage systems in the Ioannina basin and to produce a report on implementation of the Community directives on the quality of water in Greece and, in particular, the Ioannina region, which are being flagrantly infringed for the sake of profit?

Answer

The Commission has contacted the Greek authorities on several occasions on the subject of the quality of water in the Ionnina region and the specific problem of the Elbiex factory, and is investigating the matter again, with a view to preventing this pollution. Furthermore, the Commission has also initiated an infringement procedure against Greece, in accordance with Directive 91/692/EEC(16) standardizing and rationalizing reports on the implementation of certain directives relating to the environment, because the Greek reports do not comply with the directives on the quality of water.

Question No 88 by Philippe Monfils (H−0329/98)

Question No 89 by Jörn Svensson (H−0331/98)

Subject: Phare funding for an environmentally harmful project in Bulgaria

The EU has received an application for financial assistance under the Phare programme towards the construction of a chemical waste incineration plant in Sofia, Bulgaria (Project No BG 9310‐3‐03). The environmental impact of the project has not yet been assessed, and Bulgaria currently has no legislation on the incineration of waste. Bulgarian environmental organizations are fighting to stop the planned project being implemented.

What does the Commission intend to do about this project?

Will it allow the Phare programme to fund a project which does not meet the EU requirement for the environmental impact of public and private projects to be assessed before funding is granted?

Answer

At present much hazardous waste in Bulgaria is disposed of in unsuitable landfill sites, a practice which poses environmental risks such as the contamination of groundwater. Incineration of such waste, if done properly, should have a lesser environmental effect, and need not pose any threat to human health.

The Commission, through the Phare programme, agreed in principle with the Bulgarian government to fund a pilot scale incinerator as part of the ongoing process of developing an overall strategy for the treatment of chemical waste in Bulgaria. The Bulgarian government have proposed that this incinerator would be sited in Sofia.

For this and similar projects, there is a requirement that the final specifications are endorsed by the Commission. This endorsement will be conditional on compliance of these specifications with the relevant Community legislation.

The Bulgarian government are preparing for the necessary Environmental Impact Assessment into the project, and the Bulgarian environmental organizations will have the opportunity to put forward their case at the public consultation phase of this Assessment.

Question No 90 by Nuala Ahern (H−0333/98)

Subject: Shipments of high‐level waste to Japan

When the high‐level waste shipment from the French reprocessing plant at La Hague arrived at the Japanese Mutsu‐Ogawara port in Rokkasho on 10 March, the Aomori State Governor, Morio Kimura refused to let the cargo be unloaded until he had received an assurance from the Prime Minister about its long‐term safety. As there are significant quantities of HLW from La Hauge and Sellafield due to be shipped back to Japan in the future, what attempts has the Commission made to ensure that the existing reprocessing waste from Japan in Europe does not remain at the plants, with no plans for long‐term storage being available by default, because of problems of return to Japan?

Answer

The return of the high‐level waste, resulting from the reprocessing of spent fuel from Japanese nuclear power plants at La Hague (France) and Sellafield (UK), to Japan is agreed and covered by commercial contracts. It is inconceivable that the Japanese Government would refuse to allow Japanese waste to be re‐imported into Japan. It would also be contrary to Article 27.3(IV), the recently concluded Joint Convention of the safety of spent fuel management and safety of radioactive waste management. While awaiting shipment it is safely stored in a very stable form in specially designed and constructed facilities. Such facilities can be used to store high‐level waste in a vitrified form for several decades. The safety of the storage of the waste while in Europe is the responsibility of the operator of the facilities and is regulated and authorized by the national authorities in the Member States concerned.

Question No 91 by Karl Habsburg‐Lothringen (H−0339/98)

Subject: Elections in Cambodia

Elections are to take place in Cambodia in July 1998. The Commission has announced that it will provide ECU 9.5 million for this purpose. Is the Commission aware that in doing so it is turning its back on solidarity with the USA and other countries which refuse to give Hun Sen's dictatorship any pseudodemocratic legitimation? Is it true that payment of this sum of money from the European taxpayer is not conditional on the return of the democratically elected Primes Minister, Prince Ranariddh, and his active participation in the election campaign and the election itself? Does this not in itself undermine the democratic legitimation of the news government?

Answer

European Union support to the electoral process in Cambodia is subject to tight conditionality relating, among others, to the political climate during the electoral campaign and return of leaders in exile.

The Financing Agreement includes a set of special provisions reminding the authorities that Community assistance can be suspended any time if the conditions for free and fair elections are not met. The two Cambodian co‐Ministers of Interior have signed the Financing Agreement for support to the elections, on the occasion of a visit to Phnom Penh of a Delegation from the European Commission (16 January 1998).

The electoral law has been approved but its enforcement needs to be closely monitored. The participation of opposition parties needs to be permanently reassessed. The European Union position is that all political leaders who so desire, must be allowed to return to Cambodia and to run for election.

As to the return of Prince Ranariddh, this issue could not form part of the Financing Agreement (its content is merely technical). The objective of the Community's support is to bring about the conditions for free and fair elections, not to take a stand in the campaign itself; and conversely, the European Union could not oblige any political leader to run for elections. The political message during the discussions with the Royal Government of Cambodia strongly underlined the EU's view that the elections have to be considered as a pluralistic exercise that includes also the ousted Prime Minister and his followers.

In this context, the EU supports, and continues to press for, the return of Prince Ranariddh to Cambodia and his participation in the elections.

Question No 92 by Anita Pollack (H−0344/98)

Subject: International Day of the Midwife

Does the Commission commit any of its aid to India, Bangladesh, Pakistan, Nepal and Sri Lanka to the provision of safe childbirth facilities or infant mortality reduction or training for midwives? If so will it please itemize these funds.

Answer

The Commission is involved in the provision of Mother and Child Health Care (MCH) in Bangladesh and Pakistan through multi‐donors major programme addressing the whole issue of an essential package of services:

‐ Bangladesh – Health and Population Programme including ten donors under the umbrella of the World Bank. The EC component ‚Thana Improvement Pilot Project’ is centered on obstetric and Infant care. The EC supports aims, inter alia, at improving the quality and access to these basic services (18 MECU).

‐ Pakistan – The EC is now considering committing 19 MECU to the Health and Reproductive Health sector within the global Social Action Programme (SAP). MCH is the first priority of SAP in the sector. Other involvements of the EC include the financing of NGOs for services delivery in that field through both the ‚Population policy’ budget‐line and co‐financing instruments.

‐ India – In 1997 the EC launched the largest project ever funded under the PVD‐ALA development budget line for the benefit of a single developing country – a sector investment programme entitled ‚Support to Health and Family Welfare Development’. The programme provides 200 MECU over a six‐year period and is national in scope. Its main objective is the implementation of quality and of access by the broadest possible group to women's reproductive and child health services.

The programme is implemented under the umbrella of the India National Family Welfare Programme with particular emphasis on reproductive and child health, child survival and safe motherhood. The programme is in direct support of the Indian government's new five‐year strategic plan (9702) to attack poverty at the roots, in particular the poor state of women and child health.

For Nepal , the EC's involvement is so far limited to one NGO project for MHC in Palpa district. However, an identification mission is on‐going to analyze further opportunities. The regional programme ‚Asia Initiative for Reproductive Health’ (25 MECU) will include a project in Sri Lanka , focusing on youth reproductive health. The ‚Asia Initiative’ will be implemented in Pakistan, Nepal and Bangladesh as well, with the objective of developing a local NGOs network (in partnership with European NGOs) for advocacy, awareness, training services delivery in reproductive health. And also with the aim of addressing MCH issues in a comprehensive and complementary way.

Question No 93 by Niall Andrews (H−0352/98)

Subject: Europol and crime

Will the Commission outline the new role it expects Europol to play in fighting crime across Europe, including cases involving the abduction of children, once the Treaty of Amsterdam comes into force, and, in the meantime, will it state what are the consequences in the fight against crime of the present failure to complete the ratification process of the Europol Convention. Finally, can the Commission indicate what the main achievements are of Europol in its present, more limited role, particularly in the fight against illegal drugs?

Answer

The Amsterdam Treaty, to which the first part of the honourable Member's question is referring, asks the Council to promote police cooperation through Europol in general and to take, within a period of five years, measures for specific additional activities of Europol: support of specific investigative actions, including operational actions of joint teams, initiatives for investigations in the Member States, cooperation between prosecuting/investigating officials and the establishment of research and documentation networks. In the view of the Commission, these steps represent a coherent and decisive further extension of the activities of Europol. They address those crucial issues which were identified in the Action Plan against Organized Crime, as drafted by a group of high level experts from the Member States and the Commission and approved by the European Council. The Commission is convinced that these measures will lead to a more effective cooperation in the fight against organized crime.

This includes activities which are covered by the specific mandate of Europol in the fight against trade in human beings. As Europol, however, has no general mandate against kidnaping of persons, Europol is only competent for the abduction of children if this is part of the trade in human beings and if – as in all other cases for Europol – an organized structure is involved and at least two Member States are affected.

Pending the entry into force of the Amsterdam Treaty, Europol will act on the basis of the Convention, which is finally expected to enter into force in the course of this year. The Commission regrets that the ratification of the Europol Convention has not been achieved by the end of 1997, as it was, on several occasions and with full support of the Commission, endorsed by the European Council. The Convention will give Europol far more comprehensive powers in the fight against organized crime. The most important aspect is the setting up of the Europol Computer System. It enables Europol to centrally store and process data from Member States and – on the basis of agreements – from third States and organizations which will significantly improve the analysis in respect of combating crime and the effectiveness of Europol in the fight against organized crime.

Referring to the third part of the honourable Member's question it can be stated that, despite its limited mandate, the Europol Drugs Unit, which is the forerunner for Europol, has successfully been contributing to the fight against drug trafficking in the European Union. The exchange of information and intelligence via the liaison officers at EDU in drug‐related cases rose again considerably in 1997. In the limits of its remit, EDU provided analytical and coordination support to investigations in the Member States. On the basis of mandates by the Council, EDU is involved in the implementation of several projects in the fight against illicit drug trafficking as the early warning system on new synthetic drugs. EDU is also drafting annual situation reports and specific threat assessments on drug trafficking. Following other remits (illegal immigration, stolen vehicles, illicit traffic in radioactive substances and as an annex money laundering), EDU was also mandated in December 1996 with the fight against traffic in human beings which – as the competence of future Europol – partly included the abduction of children.

Question No 94 by Esko Seppänen (H−0355/98)

Subject: Environmental problems in Brussels

Various European Union institutions are located in Brussels. Many Members of the European Parliament have found to their cost that the city is not a safe place to work. Moreover, it is ecologically unsatisfactory: the city is dirty, and its effluent is evidently discharged, untreated, into a local lake.

This being the case, effluent from the European Union institutions is polluting both the lake and the North Sea.

Has the Commission drawn attention to the city's environmental problems, and will it offer its assistance in combating them?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the situation of waste water treatment and discharge in Brussels.

The Belgian region of Brussels with its 19 communities does have a sewage collection system serving most of the agglomeration, but no waste water treatment plant. The collected waste water is discharged into the river Senne without treatment, the rive Senne being a tributary to the river Schelde which flows into the North Sea.

Existing Community legislation, i.e. the Urban Waste Water Treatment Directive 91/271/EEC(17) , obliges Member States to provide all agglomerations above 2000 p.e.
*with waste water collection systems and
*with waste water treatment
(p.e. = population equivalent, a widely used measuring unit equaling the biodegradable daily pollution of one person).

The deadlines for meeting these objectives are 31.12.1998, 31.12.2000 and 31.12.2005, depending on the size of the agglomeration and the character of the receiving water.

As for Brussels, the deadline is 31.12.1998.

The plans by the Brussels region for waste water treatment foresee two waste water treatment plants for Brussels, a southern plan (treating about one third of the population's waste water) and a northern plant (treating about two thirds).

The southern plant is under construction and will be operational in 1999, the northern plant will, according to the information provided by the Belgian authorities, be operational in 2003 or 2004. Following contacts between the Commission and the Brussels authorities, the necessary measures include the construction of the waste water treatment plants, but also several main sewerage systems both in the north and in the south of Brussels.

Thus the European Commission does not consider that Belgium complies with its obligations stemming from the Urban Waste Water Treatment Directive 91/271/EEC, and in particular with Article 17, for non‐conformity of the implementation programme. It has therefore, in its role as Guardian of the Treaty, taken the necessary steps towards an infringement procedure under Article 169 of the EC Treaty.

Question No 95 by Hans Lindqvist (H−0358/98)

Subject: EU stipulation of details ruining long‐distance public transport services

The Court of Justice has rejected an application from Storstockholms Lokaltrafik (SL) – Greater Stockholm Public Transport – for a derogation from EU transport rules on the grounds that SL Buss is not a public authority but a company. As SL Buss is the only organization running services on its routes and those services are supervised by a public authority, it should be possible for the EU derogation to apply. The derogation would mean that drivers would only have to produce duty sheets for one day's driving instead of for a week. If the EU is to interfere in the details of all kinds of rules and regulations in almost every conceivable area of society, it is hardly likely that circumstances will improve for authorities, companies or individuals. Is it not time to cut through the tangled web of directives and look beyond the boundaries of Strasbourg and Brussels and realize that completely different conditions apply to public transport services in the more sparsely populated metropolitan areas, such as in Sweden and Finland, from those applying in the central parts of the EU and that Community rules in these areas are inappropriate?

Answer

In its judgment of 17 March 1998 in Case C−387/96, the Court of Justice ruled that the provision of a public passenger transport service within the framework of an exclusive contract for a predetermined period which had been obtained following a competitive tender is subject to Council Regulation (EEC) No 3820/85 of 20 December 1985 on the harmonization of certain social legislation relating to road transport(18) .

The Court is of the opinion that, in this case, the bus transport operator is in competition with professional transport operators and the derogation which may be granted to vehicles used by public authorities to provide public services which are not in competition with professional operators (Article 14.1.(b) of the Regulation) is not, therefore, applicable.

The Court did not rule on the question of whether the bus company was a public or private enterprise. The Court ruling preserves the social protection, road safety and harmonization of conditions of competition objectives of the Regulation.

Consequently, the driver of this service must carry an extract of the duty roster and a copy of the service timetable for the current day, as well as for preceding days, so that control authorities may check whether the driver has complied with his driving and resting obligations, including the weekly rest period.

As a general rule the Commission accepts – and acts upon – the point made by the honourable Member that Community rules should not be too detailed and should allow for sufficient differentiation to take into account local and regional specifities. Consistent with that, Regulation 3820/85 provides several possibilities for exceptions and derogations but these are not applicable in the present case.

Question No 96 by Ole Krarup (H−0362/98)

Subject: Danish derogation from the nitrates directive

Is the Commission planning to grant Denmark a derogation from the nitrates directive, as the Danish government has drawn up a draft law that will permit fertilizer levels in excess of those permitted by the EU directive?

Answer

On 2.3.1998 the Danish authorities informed the Commission for the second time that they intended to make use of the possibility provided for in Annex III.2(b) of the Directive to permit higher quantities of livestock manure to be spread than that stipulated in Annex III.2 of the Directive. The Commission was informed for the first time on 6.6.1997 that a derogation would be used. This was subsequently withdrawn by the Danish authorities on 21.11.1997 for further consideration.

Given the quantity of supplementary information submitted with this second notification and the necessity for the Commission to give it the attention it deserves, the examination of the justification for this derogation is currently taking place. Once this has been carried out the Commission will make a proposal to the Committee established under Article 9 of the Directive and the procedure for its consideration will follow that laid down in this Article.

Question No 97 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−0365/98)

Subject: Implications of Commission proposals on agricultural produce

The Commission's recent proposals to reform the markets in basic produce will squeeze farmers' incomes severely, intensifying the risk of bankruptcy and ruin, particularly for small family farms. The significant reductions in meat and milk prices and the derisory increase in the milk quota, the paltry increase in the oil quota, its division between the Member States and the unacceptable encumbrances on the future of the sector, the abolition of production aid, the abolition of intervention and the unacceptable method of payment, the sharp cuts in tobacco subsidies on the pretext of an anti‐smoking campaign and further cuts in cereal prices, which will not be offset by subsidies, will have a drastic impact on the entire farming world but will be even more devastating for southern producers, including Greek farmers, who have suffered repeated setbacks over the last two years through the constant reform of the COMs in Mediterranean produce.

Is the pursuit of such policies not contrary to the need to preserve the farming community and boost farmers' incomes?

Answer

The Commission shares the honourable Member"s opinion on the importance of maintaining agricultural activities and preserving the income of the agricultural population. However, it does not share the extremely negative conclusions reached by the honourable Member with regard to the effects of Agenda 2000.

In practical terms, the agricultural section of Agenda 2000 deepens and widens the 1992 reform. The experience acquired within the framework of that reform has shown that the approach adopted, particularly the partial replacement of price support by means of direct subsidies for farmers is an effective way of supporting the agricultural sector.

From 1992 to 1997, the average EC agricultural income increased by about 19 % (or 3.5 % per annum) per agricultural worker. In southern Member States, the increase was even more significant (+27.4 % or +5 % per annum). At the same time, the stabilizing effect of direct subsidies has had a positive impact, particularly in southern Member States which, during the same period, suffered several droughts, leading to very low output for arable crops.

Before the reform, in the whole of the EC, about 450 000 farmers per year gave up farming. After 1992, the number decreased, to a total of 200 000 in 1997.

On the basis of this experience, the Commission is convinced that Agenda 2000 will be an important additional step towards the consolidation of a diversified, sustainable, multi‐functional and market‐orientated agricultural sector, which also provides many social and environmental services in the rural environment.

Question No 98 by Pat Gallagher (H−0369/98)

Subject: Amsterdam Treaty referenda and public opinion

The most recent EUROBAROMETER survey showed a remarkable lack of knowledge of the substance of the Amsterdam Treaty amongst the general public. Given that referenda on this Treaty will be held in a number of Member States in the near future, including Ireland, where the survey indicated that 82 % of the electorate still 'do not know' how they will vote, can the Commission indicate what plans it has to inform the electorate about the substance of this Treaty and what conclusions it has drawn on as regards the possible need for stepping up its information activities prior to the referendum?

Answer

The Commission started a special information effort on the Amsterdam Treaty immediately after its signature. A leaflet was printed and distributed (The Treaty of Amsterdam – A citizens guide) in all the Member States.

A call for proposals was published in the Official Journal in June 1997, concerning NGO's information projects on the Treaty to be developed between January and July 1998. Three Irish projects were selected (The Irish Council of Trade Unions, the National Women's Council of Ireland and the European Movement). In the meantime, the ICTU has requested the Commission to cancel the forecast activity; on his side the Commission does not plan to intensify its information activities on the Amsterdam Treaty.

Question No 99 by Gerard Collins (H−0371/98)

Subject: Involving local groups in plans for use of structural fund resources

Given that structural fund resources need to be concentrated on projects which are capable of generating new job opportunities and economic growth in the surrounding community, can the Commission confirm that it will be inviting Member State governments to consult widely with local authorities, chambers of commerce and local sectoral groups, such as rural development groups, before those governments formulate their plans for the next round of structural fund spending and submit them to Brussels?

Answer

In its proposals for new structural funds regulations adopted on 18 March, the Commission has proposed that Member States should broaden and deepen the partnership to include local and regional authorities, economic and social partners and other relevant organizations, notably bodies dealing with environmental protection and the promotion of equal opportunities for men and women.

Local sectoral groups, such as those involved in rural development, and representatives of SMEs, including chambers of commerce, could therefore be represented within the partnership process. The Commission is also proposing that the development plans submitted by the Member States should be accompanied by the partners' opinions on them.

Question No 100 by Liam Hyland (H−0372/98)

Subject: The 'virtual cow' premium

In the Agenda 2000 proposals on CAP reform, the Commission proposes to introduce a dairy‐cow premium to compensate for cuts in the prices of dairy products. However, instead of proposing a payment based on the number of cows on a farm, the Commission is proposing payments based on 'virtual cows', using notional milk yields, thereby discriminating in favour of intensive milk producers and against the more environmentallyfriendly family farmer who uses extensive grass‐based production systems.

At a time when greater priority is being given to the encouragement of environmentally‐friendly agricultural production methods and to devising a comprehensive rural development policy, can the Commission indicate what – if any – research it can put forward to justify the 'virtual cow' proposal?

Answer

The Commission has never intended direct subsidies to discriminate against any EC milk producers, including Irish producers of course. On the contrary, the sole aim of this proposal is to enable EC producers to remain in the sector and to compete at an international level. International competition is going to increase more and more, even within the Community, due to international commitments. In this respect, the fact that a new series of international negotiations is due to start next year should not be forgotten.

More specifically, the actual price reduction will affect all milk produced under the quota system. Consequently, it is right and fair to compensate producers on the basis of what they produce, and therefore, to simplify, in proportion to their quota. In fact, this makes it possible to avoid having to record the number of cows on a farm in a given period.

Furthermore, in order to support extensive production methods, the rural development regulation includes specific measures, such as increased bonuses for changing to extensive methods and increased compensatory subsidies for under‐privileged areas.

Similarly, action in favour of the environment has been considerably strengthened. The resources allocated to agro‐environmental measures have been increased and subsidies for under‐privileged areas will be transformed into an instrument to stabilize or even promote production methods with low intermediate consumption.

Considerable efforts are being made in favour of real extensive production of beef and veal, by almost tripling the bonus allocated to that form of production. Finally, Member States must adopt specific measures to ensure compliance with environmental rules, including the reduction or withdrawal of direct subsidies.

Question No 101 by James Elles (H−0374/98)

Subject: Fusion programme

Why has the Commission decided to undertake a new evaluation of the Fusion Programme much earlier than expected and to freeze new initiatives pending the outcome of that evaluation, given that an independent, high‐level evaluation of the Fusion Programme was carried in December 1996?

Is there important new technical information which was not available to the 1996 Evaluation Board?

Will the Commission make a statement on the form, level, purpose and timetable of the review and on how it intends to ensure continuity of the Fusion Programme, in the light of its withdrawal to the end of 1999 of the fusion technology agreement which supports the European Fusion Programme in industry and associations?

Answer

In its proposal for a Council Directive on the negotiations relating to the prolongation of the four‐party ITER‐EDA agreement, the Commission indicated that it intends to commission a small group of experts to carry out a European evaluation in spring 1999. This will be done with a view to preparing a joint evaluation of the activities undertaken within the framework of prolongation. It therefore has a different institutional framework to the 1996 evaluation.

In addition, it seems that technical progress has been made since 1996. In fact, the experiments carried out on JET during the autumn of 1997 have resulted in record fusion power and energy values and have provided important information on plasma heating by fusion reaction products.

Finally, pending the Council"s adoption of the fifth Euratom framework programme, the Commission has not proposed that the NET agreement (Next European Torus) be extended beyond 31 December 1998. The next stage of the fusion activities will in fact be the implementation of the framework programme"s key “controlled thermonuclear fusion’ action, as the Commission"s proposal confirms the “reactor‐orientation’ of the activities. It also confirms that during the period 1998‐2002, the implementation of the strategy will, inter alia, require physics and fusion technology activities to be carried out in order to develop the associations" and European industry"s capacity to carry out the Next Step and prepare for its operation.

Question No 102 by Antonios Trakatellis (H−0376/98)

Subject: Compatibility of national law with Community legislation

The Greek Parliament recently enacted a law (No 2538/97) 'amending the current legislation on agricultural and veterinary pharmaceuticals, the settlement of the debts of cooperative organizations and farmers and other provisions', which contains the following:

(a) In the case of certain dairy products, olive oil, packaged fruit juices, honey and packaged meat products, it is prohibited to mix together ingredients of the same kind from a different producer country in manufacturing the products concerned, and

(b) provision is made for writing off the debts of cooperative organizations and individual farmers and providing them with financial facilities.

Are these provisions compatible with the provisions and rules of Community law? Did the Greek Government carry out the appropriate preparatory work to ensure that the Commission would accept these provisions? Could any failings in the law result in fines being imposed on the Greek Government?

Answer

Act No 2538/97, which was recently adopted by the Greek authorities, relates to foodstuffs whose composition is regulated at EC level. In fact, Directive 74/409/EEC defines the composition and marketing conditions of honey. The directive allows honey from different geographical origins to be mixed. The only restriction relates to labelling, as paragraph 4b of Article 7 of the directive states that a regional, local or topographical name must not be shown on the label unless it is the sole origin of the product.

The manufacture of fruit juices is regulated by Directive 93/77/EEC. As with honey, mixing of juices from different countries is not prohibited.

Legislation prohibiting the use of ingredients from different geographical origins in the manufacture of honey and fruit juices could therefore infringe the above‐mentioned Directives. The Greek legislation thus requires careful examination.

In what concerns the provisions for writing‐off debts of cooperative organizations and individual farmers included in Greek law No 2538/97, the Commission informs the honourable Member that, following several complaints, the Greek have transmitted to the Commission the text of the above‐mentioned law on 9 December.

This law contains a very exhaustive list of debt write‐offs allowed for more than 200 beneficiaries including cooperatives, private companies, municipalities and individual farms. The law was not notified to the Commission in advance.

The Commission is currently urgently examining the compatibility with these debt write‐offs with Community state aid rules. It will ensure that the honourable Member is informed of the outcome of this examination.

Should it transpire that State aids have been paid to individual companies in contravention of Community rules, the Commission may order recovery of the aid.

Question No 103 by Graham Watson (H−0378/98)

Subject: Ticket allocation system for the 1998 Football World Cup

How does the Commission square the CFO's proposals outlined to this House by Commissioner Van Miert at the most recent part‐session with Articles 85 and 86 of the EC Treaty?

Will the Commission report on any progress made during their most recent discussions with the CFO?

Answer

Of the 160 000 tickets which remain unsold for this year's football World Cup in France, the CFO has indicated its intention to distribute around 60 000 to supporters resident outside France whose teams are represented in the matches in question. A further 100 000 tickets will be made available to neutral supporters both within and outside France on a non‐discriminatory, first‐come‐first‐served basis.

The Commission does not believe that the arrangements proposed by the CFO contravene the competition provisions of the EC Treaty, including Articles 85 and 86. Nevertheless, given that some 570, 000 tickets have already been sold by the CFO to the general public under discriminatory arrangements which the Commission considers contrary to Article 86 of the EC Treaty, the Commission considered it essential that the CFO distributed the remaining unsold tickets exclusively to residents outside France in order to redress, as far as possible, the effects of the CFO's discriminatory behaviour.

In view of the CFO's refusal to allocate the remaining 160 000 tickets exclusively outside France, the Commission now sees no alternative but to initiate formal legal proceedings against the CFO. While having regard to the necessary procedural requirements, the Commission intends to conduct these proceedings speedily.

Question No 104 by Anne McIntosh (H−0150/98)

Subject: Kick‐back incentives to travel agents

What evidence does the Commission have of 'kick‐back' commissions and other incentives being offered by European airlines to travel agents; what action does it intend to take against airlines pursuing such anti‐competitive practices?

Answer

The Commission is currently investigating for the first time the admissibility under EC competition rules of a system of incentives made by an airline to travel agents and corporate customers.

In December 1996 the Commission opened proceedings under Article 86 of the EC Treaty against the schemes of incentives granted by British Airways to UK travel agents and large corporations. The case is investigated as a result of a complaint lodged by Virgin (case IV/34.780).

The Hearing of the interested parties took place in November 1997 and the Commission is now in the final phase of the proceedings. The decision that the Commission will take in this case will clarify the principles that airlines must respect in framing their incentive policies, in order to satisfy the requirements of Article 86 of the EC Treaty and will thus also send an important signal to other airlines.

Question No 105 by Manuel Medina Ortega (H−0181/98)

Subject: Reduction in European aid

Will the Commission say what criteria were used for reducing the aid given to the special supply scheme for the Canary Islands during 1997 and 1998? What was the size of the reduction and what impact is it likely to have on the local economy?

Answer

The reduction in the aid given to the special supply scheme for dairy products is due to changes in export refund arrangements, caused by the progressive harmonization of international and EC prices, as a result of the reform of the CAP and the conclusion of the Uruguay round. The Commission has tried to solve these difficulties, caused by events unconnected with Poseican and has managed to attenuate them.

Please note that the present level of aid will not have a negative effect on the production sector. It is with regard to liquid milk, which underwent the largest reduction in aid during the first half of 1997, that a negative economic effect on the Canary Islands" economy might be encountered, as the saving has to be passed on to the end user. Its effect would however be small because of the small percentage of the subsidy compared with the final consumer price.

Question No 106 by Marianne Eriksson (H−0220/98/rev. 1)

Subject: Employee protection when handling acrylamide

Large quantities of the sealing paste Rhoca‐Gil which contains acrylamide were used during construction of a railway tunnel through the Hallandsåsen ridge in Sweden. Acrylamide is highly neurotoxic, carcinogenic and allergenic. Medical checks showed that workers who had been in contact with this sealant had sustained injury to their nervous systems.

Parliament's resolution of 23 October 1997 (B4‐0833/97) called on the Commission to investigate the use of acrylamide in the Member States and to propose a total ban on its use where it can threaten human health or the environment or where a substitute exists.

How does the Commission view these demands?

Does it believe that further measures should be taken to prevent a repetition of what happened at Hallandsåsen?

Answer

The Commission is following the development of the incident in Sweden where a dangerous substance, Acrylamide, leached into the environment during construction of a railway tunnel. The incident is still the subject of investigation by the Swedish authorities.

According to Commission policy, restrictions on the marketing and use of dangerous substances should be based on risk assessments and analyses of costs and benefits. Acrylamide, one of the components of Rhoca‐gil, is currently the subject of risk assessment under the regulation on existing substances. As part of the risk assessment the Commission is investigating the use of Acrylamide in member States. If the risk assessment concludes that the risk posed by Acrylamide needs to be reduced, control measures at Community level will be considered. The Commission is thus responding to the parts of the parliamentary resolution cited by the honourable Member.

Member States are responsible for ensuring that the Community legislation for the control of chemicals are followed by manufacturers, distributors and importers placing chemicals on the Community market. The various directives applying to chemicals, specifically to Acrylamide, have been transposed to national law. Sweden is currently investigating the incident in Hallandsasen and will clarify whether the applicable legislation has been respected.

If it is the case that existing legislation is not sufficient to protect health and the environment, the Swedish authorities should bring this to the attention of the Commission.
